<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Captain Cook" redirects here. <p><b>James Cook</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:85%; font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (7 November 1728<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[NB 1]</a></sup> – 14 February 1779) was a British explorer, <a href="/wiki/Navigator" title="Navigator">navigator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartographer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Captain_(Royal_Navy)" title="Captain (Royal Navy)">captain</a> in the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, famous for his three voyages between 1768 and 1779 in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> and to <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> in particular. He made detailed maps of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> prior to making three voyages to the Pacific, during which he achieved the first recorded European contact with the eastern coastline of Australia and the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a>, and the first recorded circumnavigation of New Zealand.
</p><p>Cook joined the British merchant navy as a teenager and joined the Royal Navy in 1755. He saw action in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a> and subsequently surveyed and mapped much of the entrance to the <a href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_River" title="St. Lawrence River">St. Lawrence River</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Plains_of_Abraham" title="Battle of the Plains of Abraham">siege of Quebec</a>, which brought him to the attention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Admiralty" title="British Admiralty">Admiralty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>. This acclaim came at a crucial moment for the direction of British overseas exploration, and it led to his commission in 1768 as commander of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">HMS <i>Endeavour</i></a> for the first of three Pacific voyages.
</p><p>In these voyages, Cook sailed thousands of miles across largely uncharted areas of the globe. He mapped lands from New Zealand to Hawaii in the Pacific Ocean in greater detail and on a scale not previously charted by Western explorers. He surveyed and named features, and recorded islands and coastlines on European maps for the first time. He displayed a combination of seamanship, superior surveying and cartographic skills, physical courage, and an ability to lead men in adverse conditions.
</p><p>Cook <a href="/wiki/Death_of_James_Cook" title="Death of James Cook">was attacked and killed</a> in 1779 during his third exploratory voyage in the Pacific while attempting to kidnap the ruling chief of the <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">island of Hawaiʻi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalani%CA%BB%C5%8Dpu%CA%BBu" title="Kalaniʻōpuʻu">Kalaniʻōpuʻu</a>, in order to reclaim a <a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)" title="Cutter (boat)">cutter</a> taken from one of his ships after his crew took wood from a burial ground. Whilst there is controversy over Cook's role as 'an enabler of colonialism'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-2">[1]</a></sup> and the violence associated with his contacts with indigenous peoples, he left a legacy of scientific and geographical knowledge that influenced his successors well into the 20th century, and numerous memorials worldwide have been dedicated to him.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span></h2>
<p>James Cook was born on 7 November 1728 (<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">NS</a>) in the village of <a href="/wiki/Marton,_Middlesbrough" title="Marton, Middlesbrough">Marton</a> in the <a href="/wiki/North_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="North Riding of Yorkshire">North Riding of Yorkshire</a> and baptised on 14 November (N.S.) in the <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish church</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marton,_Middlesbrough#St_Cuthbert's_Church" title="Marton, Middlesbrough">St Cuthbert</a>, where his name can be seen in the church register.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby25_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby25-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> He was the second of eight children of James Cook (1693–1779), a Scottish farm labourer from <a href="/wiki/Ednam" title="Ednam">Ednam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roxburghshire" title="Roxburghshire">Roxburghshire</a>, and his locally born wife, Grace Pace (1702–1765), from <a href="/wiki/Thornaby-on-Tees" title="Thornaby-on-Tees">Thornaby-on-Tees</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby25_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby25-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup> In 1736, his family moved to Airey Holme farm at <a href="/wiki/Great_Ayton" title="Great Ayton">Great Ayton</a>, where his father's employer, Thomas Skottowe, paid for him to attend the local school. In 1741, after five years' schooling, he began work for his father, who had been promoted to farm manager. Despite not being formally educated he became capable in mathematics, astronomy and charting by the time of his <i>Endeavour</i> voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frost2018_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frost2018-7">[6]</a></sup> For leisure, he would climb a nearby hill, <a href="/wiki/Roseberry_Topping" title="Roseberry Topping">Roseberry Topping</a>, enjoying the opportunity for solitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cooks%27_Cottage" title="Cooks' Cottage">Cooks' Cottage</a>, his parents' last home, which he is likely to have visited, is now in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, Australia, having been moved from England and reassembled, brick by brick, in 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horwitz_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-horwitz-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1745, when he was 16, Cook moved 20 miles (32 km) to the fishing village of <a href="/wiki/Staithes" title="Staithes">Staithes</a>, to be apprenticed as a shop boy to grocer and <a href="/wiki/Haberdasher" title="Haberdasher">haberdasher</a> William Sanderson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby25_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby25-3">[2]</a></sup> Historians have speculated that this is where Cook first felt the lure of the sea while gazing out of the shop window.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 18 months, not proving suited for shop work, Cook travelled to the nearby port town of <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a> to be introduced to Sanderson's friends John and Henry Walker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horwitz_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-horwitz-9">[8]</a></sup> The Walkers, who were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quakers</a>, were prominent local ship-owners in the coal trade. Their house is now the <a href="/wiki/Captain_Cook_Memorial_Museum" title="Captain Cook Memorial Museum">Captain Cook Memorial Museum</a>. Cook was taken on as a merchant navy apprentice in their small fleet of vessels, plying coal along the English coast. His first assignment was aboard the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collier_(ship_type)" title="Collier (ship type)">collier</a> <i>Freelove</i>, and he spent several years on this and various other <a href="/wiki/Coastal_trading_vessel" title="Coastal trading vessel">coasters</a>, sailing between the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">Tyne</a> and London. As part of his apprenticeship, Cook applied himself to the study of algebra, geometry, trigonometry, navigation and astronomy – all skills he would need one day to command his own ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1022" data-file-width="854" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Batts_Cook.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Batts_Cook" title="Elizabeth Batts Cook">Elizabeth Cook</a>, wife and for 56 years widow of James Cook, by William Henderson, 1830</div></div></div>
<p>His three-year apprenticeship completed, Cook began working on trading ships in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>. After passing his examinations in 1752, he soon progressed through the merchant navy ranks, starting with his promotion in that year to <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_mate" title="Master's mate">mate</a> aboard the collier <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> <i>Friendship</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> In 1755, within a month of being offered command of this vessel, he volunteered for service in the Royal Navy, when Britain was re-arming for what was to become the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>. Despite the need to start back at the bottom of the naval hierarchy, Cook realised his career would advance more quickly in military service and entered the Navy at <a href="/wiki/Wapping" title="Wapping">Wapping</a> on 17 June 1755.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby27_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby27-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cook married <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Batts_Cook" title="Elizabeth Batts Cook">Elizabeth Batts</a>, the daughter of Samuel Batts, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_house#Inns" title="Public house">keeper</a> of the Bell Inn in Wapping<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> and one of his mentors, on 21 December 1762 at <a href="/wiki/St_Margaret%27s_Church,_Barking" title="St Margaret's Church, Barking">St Margaret's Church, Barking</a>, Essex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> The couple had six children: James (1763–1794), Nathaniel (1764–1780, lost aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Thunderer_(1760)" title="HMS Thunderer (1760)">HMS <i>Thunderer</i></a> which foundered with all hands in a hurricane in the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>), Elizabeth (1767–1771), Joseph (1768–1768), George (1772–1772) and Hugh (1776–1793, who died of scarlet fever while a student at <a href="/wiki/Christ%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="Christ's College, Cambridge">Christ's College, Cambridge</a>). When not at sea, Cook lived in the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a>. He attended <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Church,_Shadwell" title="St Paul's Church, Shadwell">St Paul's Church, Shadwell</a>, where his son James was baptised. Cook has no direct descendants – all of his children died before having children of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_of_Royal_Navy_career">Start of Royal Navy career</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years%27_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years' War">Great Britain in the Seven Years' War</a></div>
<p>Cook's first posting was with <a href="/wiki/HMS_Eagle_(1745)" title="HMS Eagle (1745)">HMS <i>Eagle</i></a>, serving as <a href="/wiki/Able_seaman_(rank)#Royal_Navy" title="Able seaman (rank)">able seaman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_mate" title="Master's mate">master's mate</a> under Captain Joseph Hamar for his first year aboard, and Captain <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Palliser" title="Hugh Palliser">Hugh Palliser</a> thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> In October and November 1755, he took part in <i>Eagle'</i>s capture of one French warship and the sinking of another, following which he was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Boatswain" title="Boatswain">boatswain</a> in addition to his other duties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby27_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby27-11">[10]</a></sup> His first temporary command was in March 1756 when he was briefly master of <i>Cruizer</i>, a small cutter attached to <i>Eagle</i> while on patrol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby27_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby27-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1757 Cook formally passed his <a href="/wiki/Master_(naval)" title="Master (naval)">master</a>'s examinations at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_House" title="Trinity House">Trinity House</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deptford" title="Deptford">Deptford</a>, qualifying him to navigate and handle a ship of the King's fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Williams_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-G_Williams-17">[16]</a></sup> He then joined the frigate HMS <i>Solebay</i>  as master under Captain Robert Craig.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</span></h3>
<p>During the Seven Years' War, Cook served in North America as master aboard the <a href="/wiki/Fourth-rate" title="Fourth-rate">fourth-rate</a> Navy vessel <a href="/wiki/HMS_Pembroke_(1757)" title="HMS Pembroke (1757)">HMS <i>Pembroke</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> With others in <i>Pembroke</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s crew, he took part in the major amphibious assault that captured the <a href="/wiki/Fortress_of_Louisbourg" title="Fortress of Louisbourg">Fortress of Louisbourg</a> from the French in 1758, and in the siege of <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a> in 1759. Throughout his service he demonstrated a talent for <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> and was responsible for mapping much of the entrance to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_River" title="Saint Lawrence River">Saint Lawrence River</a> during the siege, thus allowing <a href="/wiki/James_Wolfe" title="James Wolfe">General Wolfe</a> to make his famous stealth attack during the 1759 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Plains_of_Abraham" title="Battle of the Plains of Abraham">Battle of the Plains of Abraham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cook's surveying ability was also put to use in mapping the jagged coast of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> in the 1760s, aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Grenville_(1754)" title="HMS Grenville (1754)">HMS <i>Grenville</i></a>. He surveyed the northwest stretch in 1763 and 1764, the south coast between the <a href="/wiki/Burin_Peninsula" title="Burin Peninsula">Burin Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Ray" title="Cape Ray">Cape Ray</a> in 1765 and 1766, and the west coast in 1767. At this time, Cook employed local pilots to point out the "rocks and hidden dangers" along the south and west coasts. During the 1765 season, four pilots were engaged at a daily pay of 4 <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> each: John Beck for the coast west of "<a href="/wiki/St._Lawrence,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="St. Lawrence, Newfoundland and Labrador">Great St Lawrence</a>", Morgan Snook for <a href="/wiki/Fortune_Bay" title="Fortune Bay">Fortune Bay</a>, John Dawson for Connaigre and <a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Bay" title="Hermitage Bay">Hermitage Bay</a>, and John Peck for the "<a href="/wiki/Bay_d%27Espoir" title="Bay d'Espoir">Bay of Despair</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>While in Newfoundland, Cook also conducted astronomical observations, in particular of the eclipse of the sun on 5 August 1766. By obtaining an accurate estimate of the time of the start and finish of the eclipse, and comparing these with the timings at a known position in England it was possible to calculate the longitude of the observation site in Newfoundland. This result was communicated to the Royal Society in 1767.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>His five seasons in Newfoundland produced the first large-scale and accurate maps of the island's coasts and were the first scientific, large scale, hydrographic surveys to use precise <a href="/wiki/Triangulation" title="Triangulation">triangulation</a> to establish land outlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> They also gave Cook his mastery of practical surveying, achieved under often adverse conditions, and brought him to the attention of the Admiralty and Royal Society at a crucial moment both in his career and in the direction of British overseas discovery. Cook's maps were used into the 20th century, with copies being referenced by those sailing Newfoundland's waters for 200 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following on from his exertions in Newfoundland, Cook wrote that he intended to go not only "farther than any man has been before me, but as far as I think it is possible for a man to go".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Williams_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-G_Williams-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="First_voyage_.281768.E2.80.931771.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_voyage_(1768–1771)">First voyage (1768–1771)</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="First voyage of James Cook">First voyage of James Cook</a></div>
<p>On 25 May 1768,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kippis1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-kippis1-25">[24]</a></sup> the Admiralty commissioned Cook to command a scientific voyage to the Pacific Ocean. The purpose of the voyage was to observe and record the 1769 <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">transit of Venus</a> across the Sun which, when combined with observations from other places, would help to determine the distance of the Earth from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> Cook, at age 39, was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_(navy)" title="Lieutenant (navy)">lieutenant</a> to grant him sufficient status to take the command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby30_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby30-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> For its part, the Royal Society agreed that Cook would receive a one hundred <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(coin)" title="Guinea (coin)">guinea</a> gratuity in addition to his Naval pay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The expedition sailed aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">HMS <i>Endeavour</i></a>, departing England on 26 August 1768.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Cook and his crew rounded <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> and continued westward across the Pacific, arriving at <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> on 13 April 1769, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1769_Transit_of_Venus_observed_from_Tahiti" title="1769 Transit of Venus observed from Tahiti">observations of the transit</a> were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> However, the result of the observations was not as conclusive or accurate as had been hoped. Once the observations were completed, Cook opened the sealed orders, which were additional instructions from the Admiralty for the second part of his voyage: to search the south Pacific for signs of the postulated rich southern continent of <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Cook then sailed to New Zealand where he mapped the complete coastline, making only some minor errors. With the aid of <a href="/wiki/Tupaia_(navigator)" title="Tupaia (navigator)">Tupaia</a>, a Tahitian priest who had joined the expedition, Cook was the first European to communicate with the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> However, at least eight Māori were killed in violent encounters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Cook then voyaged west, reaching the southeastern coast of Australia near today's Point Hicks on 19 April 1770, and in doing so his expedition became the first recorded Europeans to have encountered its eastern coastline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[NB 2]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="473" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg/250px-Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg/375px-Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cook%27s_landing_at_Botany_Bay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cook landing at <a href="/wiki/Botany_Bay" title="Botany Bay">Botany Bay</a> (Kamay)</div></div></div><p>On 23 April, he made his first recorded direct observation of <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians" title="Aboriginal Australians">Aboriginal Australians</a> at <a href="/wiki/Brush_Island" title="Brush Island">Brush Island</a> near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bawley_Point,_New_South_Wales" title="Bawley Point, New South Wales">Bawley Point</a>, noting in his journal: "... and were so near the Shore as to distinguish several people upon the Sea beach they appear'd to be of a very dark or black Colour but whether this was the real colour of their skins or the C[l]othes they might have on I know not."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Endeavour</i> continued northwards along the coastline, keeping the land in sight with Cook charting and naming landmarks as he went. On 29 April, Cook and crew made their first landfall on the continent at a beach now known as <a href="/wiki/Silver_Beach_(New_South_Wales)" title="Silver Beach (New South Wales)">Silver Beach</a> on <a href="/wiki/Botany_Bay" title="Botany Bay">Botany Bay</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kamay_Botany_Bay_National_Park" title="Kamay Botany Bay National Park">Kamay Botany Bay National Park</a>). Two Gweagal men of the Dharawal / Eora nation opposed their landing and in the confrontation one of them was shot and wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto22_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto22-38">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Cook and his crew stayed at Botany Bay for a week, collecting water, timber, fodder and botanical specimens and exploring the surrounding area. Cook sought to establish relations with the Indigenous population without success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_date"><a href="#endnote_date">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> At first Cook named the inlet "Sting-Ray Harbour" after the many stingrays found there. This was later changed to "Botanist Bay" and finally <i>Botany Bay</i> after the unique specimens retrieved by the botanists <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Banks" title="Joseph Banks">Joseph Banks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Solander" title="Daniel Solander">Daniel Solander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> This first landing site was later to be promoted (particularly by Joseph Banks) as a suitable candidate for situating a settlement and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_colony" title="British colony">British colonial</a> outpost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:206px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="294" data-file-width="394" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg/204px-Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg/306px-Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg 2x" width="204"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_replica_in_Cooktown_harbour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HM_Bark_Endeavour_Replica" title="HM Bark Endeavour Replica"><i>Endeavour</i> replica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cooktown,_Queensland" title="Cooktown, Queensland">Cooktown, Queensland</a> harbour – anchored where the original <i>Endeavour</i> was beached for seven weeks in 1770</div></div></div><p>After his departure from Botany Bay, he continued northwards. He stopped at Bustard Bay (now known as <a href="/wiki/Seventeen_Seventy,_Queensland" title="Seventeen Seventy, Queensland">Seventeen Seventy</a>) on 23 May 1770. On 24 May, Cook and Banks and others went ashore. Continuing north, on 11 June a mishap occurred when <i>Endeavour</i> ran aground on a shoal of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>, and then "nursed into a river mouth on 18 June 1770".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> The ship was badly damaged, and his voyage was delayed almost seven weeks while repairs were carried out on the beach (near the docks of modern <a href="/wiki/Cooktown,_Queensland" title="Cooktown, Queensland">Cooktown, Queensland</a>, at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Endeavour_River" title="Endeavour River">Endeavour River</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup> The crew's encounters with the local Aboriginal people were mostly peaceful, although following a dispute over green turtles Cook ordered shots to be fired and one local was lightly wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The voyage then continued and at about midday on 22 August 1770, they reached the northernmost tip of the coast and, without leaving the ship, Cook named it York Cape (now <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> Leaving the east coast, Cook turned west and nursed his battered ship through the dangerously shallow waters of <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Strait</a>. Searching for a vantage point, Cook saw a steep hill on a nearby island from the top of which he hoped to see "a passage into the Indian Seas". Cook named the island <a href="/wiki/Possession_Island_(Queensland)" title="Possession Island (Queensland)">Possession Island</a>, where he claimed the entire coastline that he had just explored as British territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_England">Return to England</span></h3>
<p>Cook returned to England via <a href="/wiki/History_of_Jakarta" title="History of Jakarta">Batavia</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, Indonesia), where many in his crew succumbed to <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, and then the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, arriving at the island of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> on 30 April 1771.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> The ship finally returned to England on 12 July 1771, anchoring in The Downs, with Cook going to Deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interlude">Interlude</span></h3>
<p>Cook's journals were published upon his return, and he became something of a hero among the scientific community. Among the general public, however, the aristocratic botanist Joseph Banks was a greater hero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup> Banks even attempted to take command of Cook's second voyage but removed himself from the voyage before it began, and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Reinhold_Forster" title="Johann Reinhold Forster">Johann Reinhold Forster</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Georg_Forster" title="Georg Forster">Georg Forster</a> were taken on as scientists for the voyage. Cook's son George was born five days before he left for his second voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Second_voyage_.281772.E2.80.931775.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_voyage_(1772–1775)">Second voyage (1772–1775)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="2166" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg/220px-James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg/330px-James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg/440px-James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Cook%27s_portrait_by_William_Hodges.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of James Cook by <a href="/wiki/William_Hodges" title="William Hodges">William Hodges</a>, who accompanied Cook on his second voyage</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="Second voyage of James Cook">Second voyage of James Cook</a></div>
<p>Shortly after his return from the first voyage, Cook was promoted in August 1771 to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Commander_(Royal_Navy)" title="Commander (Royal Navy)">commander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> In 1772, he was commissioned to lead another scientific expedition on behalf of the Royal Society, to search for the hypothetical Terra Australis. On his first voyage, Cook had demonstrated by circumnavigating New Zealand that it was not attached to a larger landmass to the south. Although he charted almost the entire eastern coastline of Australia, showing it to be continental in size, the Terra Australis was believed to lie further south. Despite this evidence to the contrary, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Dalrymple" title="Alexander Dalrymple">Alexander Dalrymple</a> and others of the Royal Society still believed that a massive southern continent should exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cook commanded <a href="/wiki/HMS_Resolution_(1771)" title="HMS Resolution (1771)">HMS <i>Resolution</i></a> on this voyage, while <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Furneaux" title="Tobias Furneaux">Tobias Furneaux</a> commanded its companion ship, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Adventure_(1771)" title="HMS Adventure (1771)">HMS <i>Adventure</i></a>. Cook's expedition circumnavigated the globe at an extreme southern <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, becoming one of the first to cross the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circle</a> on 17 January 1773. In the Antarctic fog, <i>Resolution</i> and <i>Adventure</i> became separated. Furneaux made his way to New Zealand, where he lost some of his men during an encounter with Māori, and eventually sailed back to Britain, while Cook continued to explore the Antarctic, reaching 71°10'S on 31 January 1774.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Williams_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-G_Williams-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Cook,_English_navigator,_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_(Otaheite)_c._1773.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="3946" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/James_Cook%2C_English_navigator%2C_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_%28Otaheite%29_c._1773.jpg/250px-James_Cook%2C_English_navigator%2C_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_%28Otaheite%29_c._1773.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/James_Cook%2C_English_navigator%2C_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_%28Otaheite%29_c._1773.jpg/375px-James_Cook%2C_English_navigator%2C_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_%28Otaheite%29_c._1773.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/James_Cook%2C_English_navigator%2C_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_%28Otaheite%29_c._1773.jpg/500px-James_Cook%2C_English_navigator%2C_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_%28Otaheite%29_c._1773.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Cook,_English_navigator,_witnessing_human_sacrifice_in_Taihiti_(Otaheite)_c._1773.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration from the 1815 edition of Cook's <i>Voyages</i>, depicting Cook watching a human sacrifice in <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> c. 1773</div></div></div>
<p>Cook almost encountered the mainland of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> but turned towards Tahiti to resupply his ship. He then resumed his southward course in a second fruitless attempt to find the supposed continent. On this leg of the voyage, he brought a young Tahitian named <a href="/wiki/Omai" title="Omai">Omai</a>, who proved to be somewhat less knowledgeable about the Pacific than <a href="/wiki/Tupaia_(navigator)" title="Tupaia (navigator)">Tupaia</a> had been on the first voyage. On his return voyage to New Zealand in 1774, Cook landed at the <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Friendly Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_Island" title="Norfolk Island">Norfolk Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a>.
</p><p>Before returning to England, Cook made a final sweep across the South Atlantic from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> and surveyed, mapped, and took possession for Britain of <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a>, which had been explored by the English merchant <a href="/wiki/Anthony_de_la_Roch%C3%A9" title="Anthony de la Roché">Anthony de la Roché</a> in 1675. Cook also discovered and named <a href="/wiki/Clerke_Rocks" title="Clerke Rocks">Clerke Rocks</a> and the <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Sandwich Islands</a> ("Sandwich Land"). He then turned north to South Africa and from there continued back to England. His reports upon his return home put to rest the popular myth of Terra Australis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cook-1777.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="888" data-file-width="1269" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Cook-1777.jpg/300px-Cook-1777.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Cook-1777.jpg/450px-Cook-1777.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Cook-1777.jpg/600px-Cook-1777.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cook-1777.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>James Cook's 1777 South-Up map of <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands#South_Georgia" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a>, which he named after King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a></div></div></div>
<p>Cook's second voyage marked a successful employment of <a href="/wiki/Larcum_Kendall#K1" title="Larcum Kendall">Larcum Kendall's K1</a> copy of <a href="/wiki/John_Harrison" title="John Harrison">John Harrison</a>'s H4 <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">marine chronometer</a>, which enabled Cook to calculate his longitudinal position with much greater accuracy. Cook's log was full of praise for this time-piece which he used to make charts of the southern Pacific Ocean that were so remarkably accurate that copies of them were still in use in the mid-20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon his return, Cook was promoted to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Post-captain" title="Post-captain">post-captain</a> and given an honorary retirement from the Royal Navy, with a posting as an officer of the <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Hospital,_London" title="Greenwich Hospital, London">Greenwich Hospital</a>. He reluctantly accepted, insisting that he be allowed to quit the post if an opportunity for active duty should arise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaglehole_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaglehole-56">[54]</a></sup> His fame extended beyond the Admiralty; he was made a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Fellow of the Royal Society</a> and awarded the <a href="/wiki/Copley_Medal" title="Copley Medal">Copley Gold Medal</a> for completing his second voyage without losing a man to <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigby79_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby79-57">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Dance-Holland" title="Nathaniel Dance-Holland">Nathaniel Dance-Holland</a> painted his portrait; he dined with <a href="/wiki/James_Boswell" title="James Boswell">James Boswell</a>; he was described in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> as "the first navigator in Europe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Williams_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-G_Williams-17">[16]</a></sup> But he could not be kept away from the sea. A third voyage was planned, and Cook volunteered to find the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>. He travelled to the Pacific and hoped to travel east to the Atlantic, while a simultaneous voyage travelled the opposite route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Third_voyage_.281776.E2.80.931779.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_voyage_(1776–1779)">Third voyage (1776–1779)</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="Third voyage of James Cook">Third voyage of James Cook</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaii">Hawaii</span></h3>
<p>On his last voyage, Cook again commanded HMS <i>Resolution</i>, while Captain <a href="/wiki/Charles_Clerke" title="Charles Clerke">Charles Clerke</a> commanded <a href="/wiki/HMS_Discovery_(1774)" title="HMS Discovery (1774)">HMS <i>Discovery</i></a>. The voyage was ostensibly planned to return the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander" title="Pacific Islander">Pacific Islander</a> <a href="/wiki/Omai" title="Omai">Omai</a> to Tahiti, or so the public was led to believe. The trip's principal goal was to locate a <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a> around the American continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> After dropping Omai at Tahiti, Cook travelled north and in 1778 became the first European to begin formal contact with the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish2011_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fish2011-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collingridge_2003_380_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collingridge_2003_380-61">[59]</a></sup> After his initial landfall in January 1778 at <a href="/wiki/Waimea,_Kauai_County,_Hawaii" title="Waimea, Kauai County, Hawaii">Waimea</a> harbour, <a href="/wiki/Kauai" title="Kauai">Kauai</a>, Cook named the <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> the "Sandwich Islands" after the <a href="/wiki/John_Montagu,_4th_Earl_of_Sandwich" title="John Montagu, 4th Earl of Sandwich">fourth Earl of Sandwich</a>—the acting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lords_Commissioners_of_the_Admiralty" title="Lords Commissioners of the Admiralty">First Lord of the Admiralty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collingridge_2003_380_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Collingridge_2003_380-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h3>
<p>From the Sandwich Islands, Cook sailed north and then northeast to explore the west coast of North America north of the Spanish settlements in <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">Alta California</a>. He sighted the Oregon coast at approximately 44°30′ north latitude, naming <a href="/wiki/Cape_Foulweather" title="Cape Foulweather">Cape Foulweather</a>, after the bad weather which forced his ships south to about <a href="/wiki/43rd_parallel_north" title="43rd parallel north">43° north</a> before they could begin their exploration of the coast northward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayes_1999_42–43_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes_1999_42–43-62">[60]</a></sup> He unknowingly sailed past the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Juan_de_Fuca" title="Strait of Juan de Fuca">Strait of Juan de Fuca</a> and soon after entered <a href="/wiki/Nootka_Sound" title="Nootka Sound">Nootka Sound</a> on <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Island" title="Vancouver Island">Vancouver Island</a>. He anchored near the <a href="/wiki/First_Nations_in_Canada" title="First Nations in Canada">First Nations</a> village of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuquot,_British_Columbia" title="Yuquot, British Columbia">Yuquot</a>. Cook's two ships remained in Nootka Sound from 29 March to 26 April 1778, in what Cook called Ship Cove, now Resolution Cove,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> at the south end of <a href="/wiki/Bligh_Island_(Canada)" title="Bligh Island (Canada)">Bligh Island</a>. Relations between Cook's crew and the people of Yuquot were cordial but sometimes strained. In trading, the people of Yuquot demanded much more valuable items than the usual trinkets that had been acceptable in Hawaii. Metal objects were much desired, but the lead, pewter, and tin traded at first soon fell into disrepute. The most valuable items which the British received in trade were <a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">sea otter</a> pelts. During the stay, the Yuquot "hosts" essentially controlled the trade with the British vessels; the natives usually visited the British vessels at Resolution Cove instead of the British visiting the village of Yuquot at Friendly Cove.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>After leaving Nootka Sound in search of the Northwest Passage, Cook explored and mapped the coast all the way to the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a>, on the way identifying what came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Cook_Inlet" title="Cook Inlet">Cook Inlet</a> in Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayes_1999_42–43_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes_1999_42–43-62">[60]</a></sup> In a single visit, Cook charted the majority of the North American northwest coastline on world maps for the first time, determined the extent of Alaska, and closed the gaps in Russian (from the west) and Spanish (from the south) exploratory probes of the northern limits of the Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Williams_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-G_Williams-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Cleveley_the_Younger,_Views_of_the_South_Seas_(No._3_of_4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2422" data-file-width="3308" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/John_Cleveley_the_Younger%2C_Views_of_the_South_Seas_%28No._3_of_4%29.jpg/250px-John_Cleveley_the_Younger%2C_Views_of_the_South_Seas_%28No._3_of_4%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/John_Cleveley_the_Younger%2C_Views_of_the_South_Seas_%28No._3_of_4%29.jpg/375px-John_Cleveley_the_Younger%2C_Views_of_the_South_Seas_%28No._3_of_4%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/John_Cleveley_the_Younger%2C_Views_of_the_South_Seas_%28No._3_of_4%29.jpg/500px-John_Cleveley_the_Younger%2C_Views_of_the_South_Seas_%28No._3_of_4%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Cleveley_the_Younger,_Views_of_the_South_Seas_(No._3_of_4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HMS <i>Resolution</i> and <i>Discovery</i> in Tahiti</div></div></div>
<p>By the second week of August 1778, Cook was through the Bering Strait, sailing into the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi Sea</a>. He headed northeast up the coast of Alaska until he was blocked by sea ice at a latitude of 70°44′ north. Cook then sailed west to the <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberian</a> coast, and then southeast down the Siberian coast back to the Bering Strait. By early September 1778 he was back in the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">Bering Sea</a> to begin the trip to the Sandwich (Hawaiian) Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> He became increasingly frustrated on this voyage and perhaps began to suffer from a stomach ailment; it has been speculated that this led to irrational behaviour towards his crew, such as forcing them to eat walrus meat, which they had pronounced inedible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-seven-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Hawaii">Return to Hawaii</span></h3>
<p>Cook returned to Hawaii in 1779. After sailing around the archipelago for some eight weeks, he made landfall at <a href="/wiki/Kealakekua_Bay" title="Kealakekua Bay">Kealakekua Bay</a> on <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">Hawai'i Island</a>, largest island in the Hawaiian Archipelago. Cook's arrival coincided with the <i><a href="/wiki/Makahiki" title="Makahiki">Makahiki</a></i>, a Hawaiian <a href="/wiki/Harvest_festival" title="Harvest festival">harvest festival</a> of worship for the Polynesian god <a href="/wiki/Lono" title="Lono">Lono</a>. Coincidentally the form of Cook's ship, HMS <i>Resolution</i>, or more particularly the mast formation, sails and rigging, resembled certain significant artefacts that formed part of the season of worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-seven-66">[64]</a></sup> Similarly, Cook's clockwise route around the island of Hawaii before making landfall resembled the processions that took place in a clockwise direction around the island during the Lono festivals. It has been argued (most extensively by <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Sahlins" title="Marshall Sahlins">Marshall Sahlins</a>) that such coincidences were the reasons for Cook's (and to a limited extent, his crew's) initial <a href="/wiki/Apotheosis" title="Apotheosis">deification</a> by some Hawaiians who treated Cook as an incarnation of Lono.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahlins1985_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahlins1985-67">[65]</a></sup> Though this view was first suggested by members of Cook's expedition, the idea that any Hawaiians understood Cook to be Lono, and the evidence presented in support of it, were challenged in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-seven-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2nd_ed_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-2nd_ed-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_James_Cook" title="Death of James Cook">Death of James Cook</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg/220px-Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg/330px-Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg/440px-Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hawaii_WikiC_9015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marker at the shoreline of Kealakekua Bay near the spot Captain Cook was slain</div></div></div>
<p>After a month's stay, Cook attempted to resume his exploration of the northern Pacific. Shortly after leaving Hawaii Island, however, <i>Resolution</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s foremast broke, so the ships returned to Kealakekua Bay for repairs.
</p><p>Tensions rose, and a number of quarrels broke out between the Europeans and Hawaiians at Kealakekua Bay, including the theft of wood from a burial ground under Cook's orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks1847_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks1847-69">[67]</a></sup> An unknown group of Hawaiians took one of Cook's small boats. The evening when the cutter was taken, the people had become "insolent" even with threats to fire upon them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obeyesekere1997_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Obeyesekere1997-70">[68]</a></sup> Cook attempted to kidnap and ransom the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alii_Aimoku_of_Hawaii" title="Alii Aimoku of Hawaii">King of Hawaiʻi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalani%CA%BB%C5%8Dpu%CA%BBu" title="Kalaniʻōpuʻu">Kalaniʻōpuʻu</a>.
</p><p>The following day, 14 February 1779, Cook marched through the village to retrieve the king. Cook took the king (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali%27i" title="Ali'i">aliʻi nui</a>) by his own hand and led him away. One of Kalaniʻōpuʻu's favourite wives, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanekapolei" title="Kanekapolei">Kanekapolei</a>, and two chiefs approached the group as they were heading to the boats. They pleaded with the king not to go. An old <a href="/wiki/Kahuna" title="Kahuna">kahuna</a> (priest), chanting rapidly while holding out a coconut, attempted to distract Cook and his men as a large crowd began to form at the shore. At this point, the king began to understand that Cook was his enemy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obeyesekere1997_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Obeyesekere1997-70">[68]</a></sup> As Cook turned his back to help launch the boats, he was struck on the head by the villagers and then stabbed to death as he fell on his face in the <a href="/wiki/Breaking_wave" title="Breaking wave">surf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> He was first struck on the head with a club by a chief named <a href="/wiki/Kalaimanokaho%CA%BBowaha" title="Kalaimanokahoʻowaha">Kalaimanokahoʻowaha</a> or Kanaʻina (namesake of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Kana%27ina" title="Charles Kana'ina">Charles Kana'ina</a>) and then stabbed by one of the king's attendants, Nuaa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samwell(Jr)1791_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Samwell(Jr)1791-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> The Hawaiians carried his body away towards the back of the town, still visible to the ship through their spyglass. Four marines, Corporal James Thomas, Private Theophilus Hinks, Private Thomas Fatchett and Private John Allen, were also killed and two others were wounded in the confrontation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samwell(Jr)1791_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Samwell(Jr)1791-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png/390px-Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png/585px-Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png/780px-Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cook_Three_Voyages_59.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The routes of Captain James Cook's voyages. The first voyage is shown in <b><span style="color:red;">red</span></b>, second voyage in <b><span style="color:green;">green</span></b>, and third voyage in <b><span style="color:blue;">blue</span></b>. The route of Cook's crew following his death is shown as a dashed blue line.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span></h3>
<p>The esteem which the islanders nevertheless held for Cook caused them to retain his body. Following their practice of the time, they prepared his body with funerary rituals usually reserved for the chiefs and highest elders of the society. The body was <a href="/wiki/Disembowelment" title="Disembowelment">disembowelled</a> and baked to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Excarnation" title="Excarnation">removal of the flesh</a>, and the bones were carefully cleaned for preservation as religious icons in a fashion somewhat reminiscent of the treatment of European saints in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. Some of Cook's remains, thus preserved, were eventually returned to his crew for a formal <a href="/wiki/Burial_at_sea" title="Burial at sea">burial at sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clerke assumed leadership of the expedition and made a final attempt to pass through the Bering Strait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> He died of tuberculosis on 22 August 1779 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Gore_(Royal_Navy_captain)" title="John Gore (Royal Navy captain)">John Gore</a>, a veteran of Cook's first voyage, took command of  <i>Resolution</i> and of the expedition. <a href="/wiki/James_King_(Royal_Navy_officer)" title="James King (Royal Navy officer)">James King</a> replaced Gore in command of  <i>Discovery</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> The expedition returned home, reaching England in October 1780. After their arrival in England, King completed Cook's account of the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnographic_collections">Ethnographic collections</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/James_Cook_Collection:_Australian_Museum" title="James Cook Collection: Australian Museum">James Cook Collection: Australian Museum</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1953" data-file-width="2953" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg/220px-H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg/330px-H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg/440px-H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H000104-_Feather_Cape.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hawaiian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BBahu%CA%BBula" title="ʻahuʻula">ʻahuʻula</a> (feather cloak) held by the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Museum" title="Australian Museum">Australian Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Museum" title="Australian Museum">Australian Museum</a> acquired its "Cook Collection" in 1894 from the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_New_South_Wales" title="Government of New South Wales">Government of New South Wales</a>. At that time the collection consisted of 115 artefacts collected on Cook's three voyages throughout the Pacific Ocean, during the period 1768–80, along with documents and memorabilia related to these voyages. Many of the <a href="/wiki/Ethnography" title="Ethnography">ethnographic</a> artefacts were collected at a time of first contact between <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander" title="Pacific Islander">Pacific Peoples</a> and Europeans. In 1935 most of the documents and memorabilia were transferred to the Mitchell Library in the <a href="/wiki/State_Library_of_New_South_Wales" title="State Library of New South Wales">State Library of New South Wales</a>. The provenance of the collection shows that the objects remained in the hands of Cook's widow Elizabeth Cook, and her descendants, until 1886. In this year John Mackrell, the great-nephew of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Smith_(Royal_Navy_officer)" title="Isaac Smith (Royal Navy officer)">Isaac Smith</a>, Elizabeth Cook's cousin, organised the display of this collection at the request of the NSW Government at the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_and_Indian_Exhibition" title="Colonial and Indian Exhibition">Colonial and Indian Exhibition</a> in London. In 1887 the London-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agent-General" title="Agent-General">Agent-General</a> for the New South Wales Government, Saul Samuel, bought John Mackrell's items and also acquired items belonging to the other relatives Reverend Canon Frederick Bennett, Mrs Thomas Langton, H.M.C. Alexander, and William Adams. The collection remained with the Colonial Secretary of NSW until 1894, when it was transferred to the Australian Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomsett,_History_of_Acquisition_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomsett,_History_of_Acquisition-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation_and_science">Navigation and science</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1462" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg/290px-Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg/435px-Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg/580px-Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cooks_Karte_von_Neufundland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1775 chart of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a>, made from James Cook's <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a> surveyings</div></div></div>
<p>Cook's 12 years sailing around the Pacific Ocean contributed much to Europeans' knowledge of the area. Several islands, such as the Hawaiian group, were encountered for the first time by Europeans, and his more accurate navigational charting of large areas of the Pacific was a major achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> To create accurate maps, latitude and longitude must be accurately determined. Navigators had been able to work out latitude accurately for centuries by measuring the angle of the sun or a star above the horizon with an instrument such as a <a href="/wiki/Backstaff" title="Backstaff">backstaff</a> or <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(instrument)" title="Quadrant (instrument)">quadrant</a>. Longitude was more difficult to measure accurately because it requires precise knowledge of the time difference between points on the surface of the earth. The Earth turns a full 360 degrees relative to the sun each day. Thus longitude corresponds to time: 15 degrees every hour, or 1 degree every 4 minutes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Cook gathered accurate longitude measurements during his first voyage from his navigational skills, with the help of astronomer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Green_(astronomer)" title="Charles Green (astronomer)">Charles Green</a>, and by using the newly published <a href="/wiki/The_Nautical_Almanac" title="The Nautical Almanac"><i>Nautical Almanac</i></a> tables, via the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_distance_(navigation)" title="Lunar distance (navigation)">lunar distance</a> method – measuring the angular distance from the moon to either the sun during daytime or one of eight bright stars during night-time to determine the time at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich" title="Royal Observatory, Greenwich">Royal Observatory, Greenwich</a>, and comparing that to his local time determined via the altitude of the sun, moon, or stars.
</p><p>On his second voyage, Cook used the K1 chronometer made by <a href="/wiki/Larcum_Kendall" title="Larcum Kendall">Larcum Kendall</a>, which was the shape of a large pocket watch, 5 inches (13 cm) in diameter. It was a copy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harrison_Number_Four" title="Harrison Number Four">H4</a> clock made by <a href="/wiki/John_Harrison" title="John Harrison">John Harrison</a>, which proved to be the first to keep accurate time at sea when used on the ship <i>Deptford</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s journey to <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> in 1761–62.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> He succeeded in circumnavigating the world on his first voyage without losing a single man to <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>, an unusual accomplishment at the time. He tested several preventive measures,  most importantly the frequent replenishment of fresh food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> For presenting a paper on this aspect of the voyage to the Royal Society he was presented with the <a href="/wiki/Copley_Medal" title="Copley Medal">Copley Medal</a> in 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> Cook became the first European to have extensive contact with various people of the Pacific. He correctly postulated a link among all the Pacific peoples, despite their being separated by great ocean stretches (see <a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">Malayo-Polynesian languages</a>). Cook theorised that Polynesians originated from Asia, which scientist <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Sykes" title="Bryan Sykes">Bryan Sykes</a> later verified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> In New Zealand the coming of Cook is often used to signify the onset of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonisation" title="Colonisation">colonisation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horwitz_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-horwitz-9">[8]</a></sup>
which officially started more than 70 years after his crew became the second group of Europeans to visit that archipelago.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hodges,_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1770" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Hodges%2C_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg/290px-Hodges%2C_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Hodges%2C_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg/435px-Hodges%2C_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Hodges%2C_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg/580px-Hodges%2C_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hodges,_Resolution_and_Adventure_in_Matavai_Bay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Hodges" title="William Hodges">William Hodges'</a> painting of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Resolution_(1771)" title="HMS Resolution (1771)">HMS <i>Resolution</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Adventure_(1771)" title="HMS Adventure (1771)">HMS <i>Adventure</i></a> in <a href="/wiki/Matavai_Bay" title="Matavai Bay">Matavai Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a></div></div></div>
<p>Cook carried several scientists on his voyages; they made significant observations and discoveries. Two botanists, Joseph Banks and the Swede Daniel Solander, sailed on the first voyage. The two collected over 3,000 plant species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> Banks subsequently strongly promoted British settlement of Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> leading to the establishment of New South Wales as a penal settlement in 1788. Artists also sailed on Cook's first voyage. <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Parkinson" title="Sydney Parkinson">Sydney Parkinson</a> was heavily involved in documenting the botanists' findings, completing 264 drawings before his death near the end of the voyage. They were of immense scientific value to British botanists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collingridge_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-collingridge-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Cook's second expedition included <a href="/wiki/William_Hodges" title="William Hodges">William Hodges</a>, who produced notable landscape paintings of Tahiti, <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a>, and other locations. Several officers who served under Cook went on to distinctive accomplishments. <a href="/wiki/William_Bligh" title="William Bligh">William Bligh</a>, Cook's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sailing_master" title="Sailing master">sailing master</a>, was given command of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Bounty" title="HMS Bounty">HMS <i>Bounty</i></a> in 1787 to sail to Tahiti and return with <a href="/wiki/Breadfruit" title="Breadfruit">breadfruit</a>. Bligh became known for the <a href="/wiki/Mutiny_on_the_Bounty" title="Mutiny on the Bounty">mutiny of his crew</a>, which resulted in his being set adrift in 1789. He later became <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_South_Wales" title="Governor of New South Wales">Governor of New South Wales</a>, where he was the subject of another mutiny—the 1808 <a href="/wiki/Rum_Rebellion" title="Rum Rebellion">Rum Rebellion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Vancouver" title="George Vancouver">George Vancouver</a>, one of Cook's <a href="/wiki/Midshipman" title="Midshipman">midshipmen</a>, led a <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Expedition" title="Vancouver Expedition">voyage of exploration to the Pacific Coast of North America</a> from 1791 to 1794.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> In honour of Vancouver's former commander, his ship was named <a href="/wiki/HMS_Discovery_(1789)" title="HMS Discovery (1789)"><i>Discovery</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/George_Dixon_(Royal_Navy_officer)" title="George Dixon (Royal Navy officer)">George Dixon</a>, who sailed under Cook on his third expedition, later commanded his own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Roberts_(Royal_Navy_officer)" title="Henry Roberts (Royal Navy officer)">Henry Roberts</a>, a lieutenant under Cook, spent many years after that voyage preparing the detailed charts that went into Cook's posthumous atlas, published around 1784.
</p><p>Cook's contributions to knowledge gained international recognition during his lifetime. In 1779, while the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">American colonies</a> were <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">fighting Britain for their independence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> wrote to captains of colonial warships at sea, recommending that if they came into contact with Cook's vessel, they were to "not consider her an enemy, nor suffer any plunder to be made of the effects contained in her, nor obstruct her immediate return to England by detaining her or sending her into any other part of Europe or to America; but that you treat the said Captain Cook and his people with all civility and kindness ... as common friends to mankind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin1837_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin1837-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family,_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great,_Cambridge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2278" decoding="async" height="401" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family%2C_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great%2C_Cambridge.jpg/280px-Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family%2C_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great%2C_Cambridge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family%2C_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great%2C_Cambridge.jpg/420px-Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family%2C_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great%2C_Cambridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family%2C_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great%2C_Cambridge.jpg/560px-Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family%2C_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great%2C_Cambridge.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_tablet_%E2%80%93_Captain_James_Cook_and_his_family,_Church_of_St_Andrew_the_Great,_Cambridge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to James Cook and family in the church of <a href="/wiki/St_Andrew_the_Great" title="St Andrew the Great">St Andrew the Great</a>, Cambridge</div></div></div>
<p>A U.S. coin, the 1928 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaii_Sesquicentennial_half-dollar" title="Hawaii Sesquicentennial half-dollar">Hawaii Sesquicentennial half-dollar</a>, carries Cook's image. Minted for the 150th anniversary of his discovery of the islands, its low mintage (10,008) has made this example of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_United_States_commemorative_coins" title="Early United States commemorative coins">early United States commemorative coin</a> both scarce and expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kealakekua_Bay" title="Kealakekua Bay">site where he was killed in Hawaii</a> was marked in 1874 by a white obelisk. This land, although in Hawaii, was deeded to the United Kingdom by Princess <a href="/wiki/Likelike" title="Likelike">Likelike</a> and her husband, <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Scott_Cleghorn" title="Archibald Scott Cleghorn">Archibald Scott Cleghorn</a>, to the British Consul to Hawaii, James Hay Wodehouse, in 1877.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="article says land was bought by British consul general, and its status is unclear, not that it was deeded to the UK (April 2020)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> A nearby town is named <a href="/wiki/Captain_Cook,_Hawaii" title="Captain Cook, Hawaii">Captain Cook, Hawaii</a>; several Hawaiian businesses also carry his name. The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">Command/Service Module</a> <i>Endeavour</i> was named after Cook's ship, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">HMS <i>Endeavour</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> as was the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">Space Shuttle <i>Endeavour</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> In addition, the first Crew Dragon capsule flown by SpaceX was named for <i>Endeavour.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup>  Another shuttle, <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery"><i>Discovery</i></a>, was named after Cook's <a href="/wiki/HMS_Discovery_(1774)" title="HMS Discovery (1774)">HMS <i>Discovery</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first institution of higher education in North Queensland, Australia, was named after him, with <a href="/wiki/James_Cook_University" title="James Cook University">James Cook University</a> opening in <a href="/wiki/Townsville" title="Townsville">Townsville</a> in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> Numerous institutions, landmarks and place names reflect the importance of Cook's contributions, including the <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands" title="Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cook_Strait" title="Cook Strait">Cook Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cook_Inlet" title="Cook Inlet">Cook Inlet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cook_(crater)" title="Cook (crater)">Cook crater</a> on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aoraki_/_Mount_Cook" title="Aoraki / Mount Cook">Aoraki / Mount Cook</a>, the highest summit in New Zealand, is named for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> Another <a href="/wiki/Mount_Cook_(Saint_Elias_Mountains)" title="Mount Cook (Saint Elias Mountains)">Mount Cook</a> is on the border between the U.S. state of Alaska and the Canadian <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a> territory, and is designated Boundary Peak 182 as one of the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Boundary_Peaks_of_the_Alaska_%E2%80%93_British_Columbia/Yukon_border" title="List of Boundary Peaks of the Alaska – British Columbia/Yukon border">Boundary
